<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_technetium" title="Template:Infobox technetium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_technetium" title="Template talk:Infobox technetium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_technetium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Technetium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Tc</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 43. It is the lightest element whose isotopes are all <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>; none are <a href="/wiki/Stable_nuclide" title="Stable nuclide">stable</a>, excluding the fully ionized state of <sup>97</sup>Tc.<sup id="cite_ref-Takahashi_et_al_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takahashi_et_al-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Nearly all technetium is produced synthetically, and only about 18000 tons can be found at any given time in the Earth's crust. Naturally occurring technetium is a spontaneous <a href="/wiki/Fission_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission product">fission product</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uranium_ore" title="Uranium ore">uranium ore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> ore, the most common source, or the product of <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> in <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> ores. This silvery gray, crystalline <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> lies between <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> in <a href="/wiki/Group_7_element" title="Group 7 element">group 7</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, and its chemical properties are intermediate between those of these two adjacent elements. The most common naturally occurring isotope is <sup>99</sup>Tc.
</p><p>Many of technetium's properties were predicted by <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a> before the element was discovered. Mendeleev noted a gap in his periodic table and gave the undiscovered element the provisional name <i><a href="/wiki/Mendeleev%27s_predicted_elements" title="Mendeleev&#39;s predicted elements">ekamanganese</a></i> (<i>Em</i>). In 1937, technetium (specifically the <a href="/wiki/Technetium-97" class="mw-redirect" title="Technetium-97">technetium-97</a> isotope) became the first predominantly artificial element to be produced, hence its name (from the Greek <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">τεχνητός</span>, meaning "synthetic or artificial", + <span class="nowrap"><i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-ium#Suffix" class="extiw" title="wikt:-ium">-ium</a></i>).</span>
</p><p>One short-lived <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a>-emitting <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomer</a> of technetium—<a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">technetium-99m</a>—is used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a> for a wide variety of diagnostic tests, such as bone cancer diagnoses. The ground state of this <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a>, technetium-99, is used as a gamma-ray-free source of <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particles</a>. Long-lived <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_technetium" title="Isotopes of technetium">technetium isotopes</a> produced commercially are by-products of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> and are extracted from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel rods</a>. Because no isotope of technetium has a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> longer than 4.2&#160;million years (<a href="/wiki/Technetium-98" class="mw-redirect" title="Technetium-98">technetium-98</a>), the 1952 detection of technetium in <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a>, helped to prove that stars can produce heavier elements.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_for_element_43">Search for element 43</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Search for element 43">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the 1860s through 1871, early forms of the periodic table proposed by Dmitri Mendeleev contained a gap between <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> (element&#160;42) and <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a> (element&#160;44). In 1871, Mendeleev predicted this missing element would occupy the empty place below <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> and have similar chemical properties. Mendeleev gave it the provisional name <i>ekamanganese</i> (from <i>eka</i>-, the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word for <i>one)</i> because the predicted element was one place down from the known element manganese.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_misidentifications">Early misidentifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early misidentifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many early researchers, both before and after the periodic table was published, were eager to be the first to discover and name the missing element. Its location in the table suggested that it should be easier to find than other undiscovered elements.
</p>

<td><a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">Rhenium</a>, which was the then unknown dvi-manganese<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irreproducible_results">Irreproducible results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Irreproducible results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_(1904-1945,_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg/220px-Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg/330px-Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg/440px-Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_%281904-1945%2C_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Periodisches_System_der_Elemente_(1904-1945,_now_Gdansk_University_of_Technology).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Periodisches System der Elemente (1904–1945, now at the <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk_University_of_Technology" title="Gdańsk University of Technology">Gdańsk University of Technology</a>): lack of elements: 84 polonium Po (though discovered as early as in 1898 by <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Maria Sklodowska-Curie</a>), 85 astatine At (1940, in Berkeley), 87 francium Fr (1939, in France), 93 neptunium Np (1940, in Berkeley) and other actinides and lanthanides. Old symbols for: 18 argon Ar (here: A), 43 technetium Tc (Ma, masurium, 1925, dismissed as an error and finally confirmed in 1937, Palermo), 54 xenon Xe (X), 86 radon, Rn (Em, emanation)</div></div></div>
<p>German chemists <a href="/wiki/Walter_Noddack" title="Walter Noddack">Walter Noddack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Berg_(scientist)" title="Otto Berg (scientist)">Otto Berg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ida_Tacke" class="mw-redirect" title="Ida Tacke">Ida Tacke</a> reported the discovery of element&#160;75 and element&#160;43 in 1925, and named element&#160;43 <i><a href="/wiki/Masurium" class="mw-redirect" title="Masurium">masurium</a></i> (after <a href="/wiki/Masuria" title="Masuria">Masuria</a> in eastern <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, now in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, the region where Walter Noddack's family originated).<sup id="cite_ref-multidict_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-multidict-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The group bombarded <a href="/wiki/Ferrocolumbite" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferrocolumbite">columbite</a> with a beam of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> and deduced element&#160;43 was present by examining <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> diffraction <a href="/wiki/Spectrogram" title="Spectrogram">spectrograms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p423_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p423-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of the X-rays produced is related to the atomic number by a formula derived by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moseley" title="Henry Moseley">Henry Moseley</a> in 1913. The team claimed to detect a faint X-ray signal at a wavelength produced by element&#160;43. Later experimenters could not replicate the discovery, and it was dismissed as an error for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-armstrong_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armstrong-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Still, in 1933, a series of articles on the discovery of elements quoted the name <i>masurium</i> for element&#160;43.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> Whether the 1925 team actually did discover element&#160;43 is still debated.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_discovery_and_later_history">Official discovery and later history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Official discovery and later history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_chemical_elements" class="mw-redirect" title="Discovery of the chemical elements">discovery</a> of element&#160;43 was finally confirmed in a 1937 experiment at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Palermo" title="University of Palermo">University of Palermo</a> in Sicily by <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Perrier" title="Carlo Perrier">Carlo Perrier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Segr%C3%A8" title="Emilio Segrè">Emilio Segrè</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992p164_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992p164-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In mid-1936, Segrè visited the United States, first <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> in New York and then the <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Berkeley_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory">Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory</a> in California. He persuaded <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a> inventor <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Lawrence" title="Ernest Lawrence">Ernest Lawrence</a> to let him take back some discarded cyclotron parts that had become <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>. Lawrence mailed him a <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> foil that had been part of the deflector in the cyclotron.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Segrè enlisted his colleague Perrier to attempt to prove, through comparative chemistry, that the molybdenum activity was indeed from an element with the atomic number 43. In 1937 they succeeded in isolating the <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> <a href="/wiki/Technetium-95" class="mw-redirect" title="Technetium-95">technetium-95m</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technetium-97" class="mw-redirect" title="Technetium-97">technetium-97</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-segre_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-segre-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-blocks_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> University of Palermo officials wanted them to name their discovery "<i>panormium</i>", after the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name for <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>, <i>Panormus</i>. In 1947<sup id="cite_ref-segre_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-segre-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> element&#160;43 was named after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word <i>τεχνητός</i>, meaning "artificial", since it was the first element to be artificially produced.<sup id="cite_ref-history-origin_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-origin-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-multidict_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-multidict-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Segrè returned to Berkeley and met <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a>. They isolated the <a href="/wiki/Metastable_isotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Metastable isotope">metastable isotope</a> <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">technetium-99m</a>, which is now used in some ten million medical diagnostic procedures annually.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1952, astronomer <a href="/wiki/Paul_W._Merrill" title="Paul W. Merrill">Paul W. Merrill</a> in California detected the <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">spectral signature</a> of technetium (specifically <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of 403.1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>, 423.8&#160;nm, 426.2&#160;nm, and 429.7&#160;nm) in light from <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification#Class_S" title="Stellar classification">S-type</a> <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The stars were near the end of their lives, yet were rich in this short-lived element, indicating that it was being produced in the stars by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a>. This evidence bolstered the hypothesis that heavier elements are the product of <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> in stars.<sup id="cite_ref-blocks_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> More recently, such observations provided evidence that elements are formed by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-s8_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since that discovery, there have been many searches in terrestrial materials for natural sources of technetium. In 1962, technetium-99 was isolated and identified in <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">pitchblende</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">Belgian Congo</a> in extremely small quantities (about 0.2&#160;ng/kg);<sup id="cite_ref-s8_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> there it originates as a <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a> product of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a> <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactor</a> contains evidence that significant amounts of technetium-99 were produced and have since decayed into <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium-99" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruthenium-99">ruthenium-99</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-s8_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium is a silvery-gray radioactive <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> with an appearance similar to <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, commonly obtained as a gray powder.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a> of the pure metal is <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_crystal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Hexagonal crystal system">hexagonal</a> <a href="/wiki/Close-packed" class="mw-redirect" title="Close-packed">close-packed</a>. Atomic technetium has characteristic <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">emission lines</a> at these <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of light: 363.3&#160;<a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>, 403.1&#160;nm, 426.2&#160;nm, 429.7&#160;nm, and 485.3&#160;nm.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The metal form is slightly <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a>, meaning its <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">magnetic dipoles</a> align with external <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>, but will assume random orientations once the field is removed.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Pure, metallic, single-crystal technetium becomes a <a href="/wiki/Type-II_superconductor" title="Type-II superconductor">type-II superconductor</a> at temperatures below 7.46&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Below this temperature, technetium has a very high <a href="/wiki/London_penetration_depth" title="London penetration depth">magnetic penetration depth</a>, greater than any other element except <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium is located in the <a href="/wiki/Group_7_element" title="Group 7 element">seventh group</a> of the periodic table, between <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>. As predicted by the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_periodic_table" title="History of the periodic table">periodic law</a>, its chemical properties are between those two elements. Of the two, technetium more closely resembles rhenium, particularly in its chemical inertness and tendency to form <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Unlike manganese, technetium does not readily form <a href="/wiki/Cation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cation">cations</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> with a net positive charge). Technetium exhibits nine <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> from −1 to +7, with +4, +5, and +7 being the most common.<sup id="cite_ref-LANL_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Technetium dissolves in <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, and concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, but it is not soluble in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> of any concentration.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Metallic technetium slowly <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishes</a> in moist air<sup id="cite_ref-LANL_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and, in powder form, burns in <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.
</p><p>Technetium can catalyse the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> by <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, and this property is due to its multiplicity of valencies.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This caused a problem in the separation of plutonium from uranium in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">nuclear fuel processing</a>, where hydrazine is used as a protective reductant to keep plutonium in the trivalent rather than the more stable tetravalent state. The problem was exacerbated by the mutually-enhanced solvent extraction of technetium and zirconium at the previous stage,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and required a process modification.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pertechnetate_and_derivatives">Pertechnetate and derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Pertechnetate and derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pertechnetate1.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pertechnetate1.svg/200px-Pertechnetate1.svg.png" width="200" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pertechnetate1.svg/300px-Pertechnetate1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pertechnetate1.svg/400px-Pertechnetate1.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="125" data-file-height="96" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pertechnetate1.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pertechnetate is one of the most available forms of technetium.  It is structurally related to <a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate">permanganate</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The most prevalent form of technetium that is easily accessible is sodium pertechnetate, Na[TcO<sub>4</sub>].  The majority of this material is produced by radioactive decay from [<sup>99</sup>MoO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>:<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nuclmed_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuclmed-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>[<sup>99</sup>MoO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>  →   [<sup>99</sup>TcO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup>  +  γ</dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pertechnetate" title="Pertechnetate">Pertechnetate</a> (tetroxidotechnetate) <span class="chemf nowrap">TcO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> behaves analogously to perchlorate, with which it is isostructural.  It is <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Tetrahedral molecular geometry">tetrahedral</a>. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate">permanganate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">MnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), it is only a weak oxidizing agent.
</p><p>Related to pertechnetate is <a href="/wiki/Technetium(VII)_oxide" title="Technetium(VII) oxide">heptoxide</a>.  This pale-yellow, volatile solid is produced by oxidation of Tc metal and related precursors:
</p>
<dl><dd>4 Tc + 7 O<sub>2</sub> → 2 Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub></dd></dl>
<p>It is a very rare example of a molecular metal oxide, other examples being <a href="/wiki/OsO4" class="mw-redirect" title="OsO4">OsO<sub>4</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/RuO4" class="mw-redirect" title="RuO4">RuO<sub>4</sub></a>.  It adopts a <a href="/wiki/Centrosymmetry" title="Centrosymmetry">centrosymmetric</a> structure with two types of Tc−O bonds with 167 and 184&#160;pm bond lengths.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium heptoxide hydrolyzes to <a href="/wiki/Pertechnetate" title="Pertechnetate">pertechnetate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pertechnetic_acid" title="Pertechnetic acid">pertechnetic acid</a>, depending on the pH:<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<dl><dd><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dd>Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> + 2 OH<sup>−</sup> →  2 TcO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
<dd>Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O  →  2 HTcO<sub>4</sub></dd></dl>
<p>HTcO<sub>4</sub> is a strong acid. In concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, [TcO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup> converts to the octahedral form TcO<sub>3</sub>(OH)(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>, the conjugate base of the hypothetical tri<a href="/wiki/Aquo_complex" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquo complex">aquo complex</a> [TcO<sub>3</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub>]<sup>+</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_chalcogenide_derivatives">Other chalcogenide derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other chalcogenide derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium forms a dioxide,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Metal_dichalcogenide" class="mw-redirect" title="Metal dichalcogenide">disulfide</a>, di<a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenide</a>, and di<a href="/wiki/Telluride_(chemistry)" title="Telluride (chemistry)">telluride</a>.  An ill-defined Tc<sub>2</sub>S<sub>7</sub> forms upon treating <a href="/wiki/Pertechnate" class="mw-redirect" title="Pertechnate">pertechnate</a> with hydrogen sulfide. It thermally decomposes into disulfide and elemental sulfur.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  Similarly the dioxide can be produced by reduction of the Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.
</p><p>Unlike the case for rhenium, a trioxide has not been isolated for technetium. However, TcO<sub>3</sub> has been identified in the gas phase using <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_hydride_and_halide_complexes">Simple hydride and halide complexes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Simple hydride and halide complexes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium forms the simple complex <span class="chemf nowrap">TcH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span></span>.  The potassium salt is <a href="/wiki/Isostructural" title="Isostructural">isostructural</a> with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nonahydridorhenate" title="Potassium nonahydridorhenate"><span class="chemf nowrap">ReH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span></span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The following binary (containing only two elements) technetium halides are known: <a href="/wiki/TcF6" class="mw-redirect" title="TcF6">TcF<sub>6</sub></a>, TcF<sub>5</sub>, <a href="/wiki/TcCl4" class="mw-redirect" title="TcCl4">TcCl<sub>4</sub></a>, TcBr<sub>4</sub>, TcBr<sub>3</sub>, α-TcCl<sub>3</sub>, β-TcCl<sub>3</sub>, TcI<sub>3</sub>, α-TcCl<sub>2</sub>, and β-TcCl<sub>2</sub>.  The <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> range from Tc(VI) to Tc(II). Technetium halides exhibit different structure types, such as molecular octahedral complexes, extended chains, layered sheets, and metal clusters arranged in a three-dimensional network.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AS_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AS-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> These compounds are produced by combining the metal and halogen or by less direct reactions.
</p><p>TcCl<sub>4</sub> is obtained by chlorination of Tc metal or Tc<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> Upon heating, TcCl<sub>4</sub> gives the corresponding Tc(III) and Tc(II) chlorides.<sup id="cite_ref-AS_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AS-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>TcCl<sub>4</sub> → α-TcCl<sub>3</sub> + 1/2 Cl<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd>TcCl<sub>3</sub> → β-TcCl<sub>2</sub> + 1/2 Cl<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png/180px-Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png" width="180" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png/270px-Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png/360px-Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="791" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zirconium-tetrachloride-3D-balls-A.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>TcCl<sub>4</sub> forms chain-like structures, similar to the behavior of several other metal tetrachlorides.</div></div></div>
<p>The structure of TcCl<sub>4</sub> is composed of infinite zigzag chains of edge-sharing TcCl<sub>6</sub> octahedra.  It is isomorphous to transition metal tetrachlorides of <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hafnium" title="Hafnium">hafnium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AS_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AS-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two polymorphs of technetium trichloride exist, α- and β-TcCl<sub>3</sub>. The α polymorph is also denoted as Tc<sub>3</sub>Cl<sub>9</sub>. It adopts a confacial <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry#Bioctahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">bioctahedral structure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>  It is prepared by treating the chloro-acetate Tc<sub>2</sub>(O<sub>2</sub>CCH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub> with HCl. Like <a href="/wiki/Trirhenium_nonachloride" title="Trirhenium nonachloride">Re<sub>3</sub>Cl<sub>9</sub></a>, the structure of the α-polymorph consists of triangles with short M-M distances. β-TcCl<sub>3</sub> features octahedral Tc centers, which are organized in pairs, as seen also for <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum_trichloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Molybdenum trichloride">molybdenum trichloride</a>. TcBr<sub>3</sub> does not adopt the structure of either trichloride phase.  Instead it has the structure of <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum_tribromide" class="mw-redirect" title="Molybdenum tribromide">molybdenum tribromide</a>, consisting of chains of confacial octahedra with alternating short and long Tc—Tc contacts.  TcI<sub>3</sub> has the same structure as the high temperature phase of <a href="/wiki/Titanium(III)_iodide" title="Titanium(III) iodide">TiI<sub>3</sub></a>, featuring chains of confacial octahedra with equal Tc—Tc contacts.<sup id="cite_ref-AS_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AS-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several anionic technetium halides are known.  The binary tetrahalides can be converted to the hexahalides [TcX<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2−</sup> (X = F, Cl, Br, I), which adopt <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">octahedral molecular geometry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-s8_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>  More reduced halides form anionic clusters with Tc–Tc bonds.  The situation is similar for the related elements of Mo, W, Re.  These clusters have the nuclearity Tc<sub>4</sub>, Tc<sub>6</sub>, Tc<sub>8</sub>, and Tc<sub>13</sub>. The more stable Tc<sub>6</sub> and Tc<sub>8</sub> clusters have prism shapes where vertical pairs of Tc atoms are connected by triple bonds and the planar atoms by single bonds. Every technetium atom makes six bonds, and the remaining valence electrons can be saturated by one axial and two <a href="/wiki/Bridging_ligand" title="Bridging ligand">bridging ligand</a> halogen atoms such as <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_and_organometallic_complexes">Coordination and organometallic complexes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Coordination and organometallic complexes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tc_CNCH2CMe2(OMe)_6Cation.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png/220px-Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png" width="220" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png/330px-Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png/440px-Tc_CNCH2CMe2%28OMe%29_6Cation.png 2x" data-file-width="1018" data-file-height="961" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tc_CNCH2CMe2(OMe)_6Cation.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Technetium_(99mTc)_sestamibi" title="Technetium (99mTc) sestamibi">Technetium (99mTc) sestamibiis</a> ("Cardiolite") widely used for imaging of the heart.</div></div></div>
<p>Technetium forms a variety of <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complexes</a> with organic ligands.  Many have been well-investigated because of their relevance to nuclear medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium forms a variety of compounds with Tc–C bonds, i.e. organotechnetium complexes.  Prominent members of this class are complexes with CO, arene, and cyclopentadienyl ligands.<sup id="cite_ref-Alberto_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alberto-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  The binary carbonyl Tc<sub>2</sub>(CO)<sub>10</sub> is a white volatile solid.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In this molecule, two technetium atoms are bound to each other; each atom is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Octahedron" title="Octahedron">octahedra</a> of five carbonyl ligands. The bond length between technetium atoms, 303&#160;pm,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> is significantly larger than the distance between two atoms in metallic technetium (272&#160;pm). Similar <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbonyl">carbonyls</a> are formed by technetium's <a href="/wiki/Congener_(chemistry)" title="Congener (chemistry)">congeners</a>, manganese and rhenium.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>  Interest in organotechnetium compounds has also been motivated by applications in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">nuclear medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alberto_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alberto-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Unusual for other metal carbonyls, Tc forms aquo-carbonyl complexes, prominent being [Tc(CO)<sub>3</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub>]<sup>+</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alberto_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alberto-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_technetium" title="Isotopes of technetium">Isotopes of technetium</a></div>
<p>Technetium, with <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> (denoted <i>Z</i>) 43, is the lowest-numbered element in the periodic table of which all isotopes are <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>. The second-lightest exclusively radioactive element, <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>, has an atomic number of 61.<sup id="cite_ref-LANL_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">Atomic nuclei</a> with an odd number of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> are less stable than those with even numbers, even when the total number of <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a> (protons + <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>) is even,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> and odd numbered elements have fewer stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>.
</p><p>The most stable <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radioactive isotopes</a> are technetium-98 with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 4.2&#160;million&#160;years (<a href="/wiki/Annum" class="mw-redirect" title="Annum">Ma</a>), technetium-97 with 2.6&#160;Ma, and technetium-99 with 211,000&#160;years.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Thirty other radioisotopes have been characterized with <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass numbers</a> ranging from 85 to 118.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Most of these have half-lives that are less than an hour, the exceptions being technetium-93 (half-life: 2.73&#160;hours), technetium-94 (half-life: 4.88&#160;hours), technetium-95 (half-life: 20&#160;hours), and technetium-96 (half-life: 4.3&#160;days).<sup id="cite_ref-CRCisotopes_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRCisotopes-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary <a href="/wiki/Decay_mode" class="mw-redirect" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> for isotopes lighter than technetium-98 (<sup>98</sup>Tc) is <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> (<i>Z</i>&#160;=&#160;42).<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> For technetium-98 and heavier isotopes, the primary mode is <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta emission</a> (the emission of an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>), producing <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a> (<i>Z</i>&#160;=&#160;44), with the exception that technetium-100 can decay both by beta emission and electron capture.<sup id="cite_ref-NNDC_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium also has numerous <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a>, which are isotopes with one or more <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited</a> nucleons. Technetium-97m (<sup>97m</sup>Tc; 'm' stands for <a href="/wiki/Metastability" title="Metastability">metastability</a>) is the most stable, with a half-life of 91&#160;days (0.0965&#160;MeV).<sup id="cite_ref-CRCisotopes_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRCisotopes-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> This is followed by technetium-95m (half-life: 61&#160;days, 0.03&#160;MeV), and technetium-99m (half-life: 6.01 hours, 0.142&#160;MeV).<sup id="cite_ref-CRCisotopes_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRCisotopes-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Technetium-99m emits only <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> and decays to technetium-99.<sup id="cite_ref-CRCisotopes_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRCisotopes-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium-99 (<sup>99</sup>Tc) is a major product of the fission of uranium-235 (<sup>235</sup>U), making it the most common and most readily available isotope of technetium. One gram of technetium-99 produces 6.2×10<sup>8</sup>&#160;disintegrations a second (that is, 0.62&#160;G<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a>/g).<sup id="cite_ref-enc_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Occurrence and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Technetium occurs naturally in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> in minute concentrations of about 0.003 parts per trillion. This totals about 18000 tonnes at any given time, assuming the mass of the Earth's crust is <span style="display:none;">6000000000000000000000</span>6×10<sup>21</sup> kilograms. Technetium is so rare because technetium-98's <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> is only 4.2&#160;million&#160;years. More than a thousand of such periods have passed since the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, so the probability for the survival of even one atom of <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a> technetium is effectively zero. However, small amounts exist as spontaneous <a href="/wiki/Fission_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission product">fission products</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uranium_ore" title="Uranium ore">uranium ores</a>. A kilogram of uranium contains an estimated 1&#160;nanogram (10<sup>−9</sup>&#160;g) of technetium.<sup id="cite_ref-blocks_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> stars with the spectral types S-, M-, and N contain a spectral absorption line indicating the presence of technetium.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> These red-giants are known informally as <a href="/wiki/Technetium_star" title="Technetium star">technetium stars</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission_waste_product">Fission waste product</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Fission waste product">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In contrast to the rare natural occurrence, bulk quantities of technetium-99 are produced each year from <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel rods</a>, which contain various fission products. The fission of a gram of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> yields 27&#160;mg of technetium-99, giving technetium a <a href="/wiki/Fission_product_yield" title="Fission product yield">fission product yield</a> of 6.1%.<sup id="cite_ref-enc_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Fissile" class="mw-redirect" title="Fissile">fissile</a> isotopes produce similar yields of technetium, such as 4.9% from <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">uranium-233</a> and 6.21% from <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  An estimated 49,000&#160;T<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a> (78&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">metric tons</a>) of technetium was produced in nuclear reactors between 1983 and 1994, by far the dominant source of terrestrial technetium.<sup id="cite_ref-yoshihara_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoshihara-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-leon_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leon-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Only a fraction of the production is used commercially.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Technetium-99 is produced by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> of both uranium-235 and plutonium-239. It is therefore present in <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive waste</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">nuclear fallout</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">fission bomb</a> explosions. Its decay, measured in <a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">becquerels</a> per amount of spent fuel, is the dominant contributor to nuclear waste radioactivity after about 10<sup>4</sup> to 10<sup>6</sup>&#160;years after the creation of the nuclear waste.<sup id="cite_ref-yoshihara_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoshihara-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> From 1945 to 1994, an estimated 160&#160;T<a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a> (about 250&#160;kg) of technetium-99 was released into the environment during atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear test">nuclear tests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-yoshihara_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoshihara-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The amount of technetium-99 from nuclear reactors released into the environment up to 1986 is on the order of 1000&#160;TBq (about 1600&#160;kg), primarily by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_reprocessing" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear fuel reprocessing">nuclear fuel reprocessing</a>; most of this was discharged into the sea. Reprocessing methods have reduced emissions since then, but as of 2005 the primary release of technetium-99 into the environment is by the <a href="/wiki/Sellafield" title="Sellafield">Sellafield</a> plant, which released an estimated 550&#160;TBq (about 900&#160;kg) from 1995–1999 into the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-leon_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leon-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> From 2000 onwards the amount has been limited by regulation to 90&#160;TBq (about 140&#160;kg) per year.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>  Discharge of technetium into the sea resulted in contamination of some seafood with minuscule quantities of this element. For example, <a href="/wiki/European_lobster" class="mw-redirect" title="European lobster">European lobster</a> and fish from west <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a> contain about 1&#160;Bq/kg of technetium.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission_product_for_commercial_use">Fission product for commercial use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fission product for commercial use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Metastability" title="Metastability">metastable</a> isotope technetium-99m is continuously produced as a <a href="/wiki/Fission_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission product">fission product</a> from the fission of uranium or <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>:
</p>
