  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/33a9d13cb741fa5c8efce11540847d7deac9654b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.079ex; margin-top: -0.415ex; margin-bottom: -0.592ex; width:62.84ex; height:6.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {^{99}_{39}Y -&gt;[\beta^-][1.47\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{40}Zr -&gt;[\beta^-][2.1\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{41}Nb -&gt;[\beta^-][15.0\,{\ce {s}}] ^{99}_{42}Mo -&gt;[\beta^-][65.94\,{\ce {h}}] ^{99}_{43}Tc -&gt;[\beta^-][211,100\,{\ce {y}}] ^{99}_{44}Ru}}}"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Because used fuel is allowed to stand for several years before reprocessing, all molybdenum-99 and technetium-99m is decayed by the time that the fission products are separated from the major <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> in conventional <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">nuclear reprocessing</a>. The liquid left after plutonium–uranium extraction (<a href="/wiki/PUREX" title="PUREX">PUREX</a>) contains a high concentration of technetium as <span class="chemf nowrap">TcO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> but almost all of this is technetium-99, not technetium-99m.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The vast majority of the technetium-99m used in medical work is produced by irradiating dedicated <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium#Highly_enriched_uranium_(HEU)" title="Enriched uranium">highly enriched uranium</a> targets in a reactor, extracting molybdenum-99 from the targets in reprocessing facilities,<sup id="cite_ref-nuclmed_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuclmed-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and recovering at the diagnostic center the technetium-99m produced upon decay of molybdenum-99.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NAS_Report_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAS_Report-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Molybdenum-99 in the form of molybdate <span class="chemf nowrap">MoO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorbed</a> onto acid alumina (<span class="chemf nowrap">Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) in a <a href="/wiki/Radiation_shielding" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiation shielding">shielded</a> <a href="/wiki/Column_chromatography" title="Column chromatography">column chromatograph</a> inside a <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m_generator" title="Technetium-99m generator">technetium-99m generator</a> ("technetium cow", also occasionally called a "molybdenum cow"). Molybdenum-99 has a half-life of 67&#160;hours, so short-lived technetium-99m (half-life: 6&#160;hours), which results from its decay, is being constantly produced.<sup id="cite_ref-blocks_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The soluble <a href="/wiki/Pertechnetate" title="Pertechnetate">pertechnetate</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">TcO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> can then be chemically extracted by <a href="/wiki/Elution" title="Elution">elution</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Saline_solution" class="mw-redirect" title="Saline solution">saline solution</a>. A drawback of this process is that it requires targets containing uranium-235, which are subject to the security precautions of fissile materials.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg/220px-First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg/330px-First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg/440px-First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1392" data-file-height="1788" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_technetium-99m_generator_-_1958.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first technetium-99m generator, unshielded, 1958. A Tc-99m <a href="/wiki/Pertechnetate" title="Pertechnetate">pertechnetate</a> solution is being eluted from Mo-99 <a href="/wiki/Molybdate" title="Molybdate">molybdate</a> bound to a chromatographic substrate</div></div></div>
<p>Almost two-thirds of the world's supply comes from two reactors; the <a href="/wiki/National_Research_Universal_Reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="National Research Universal Reactor">National Research Universal Reactor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Chalk_River_Laboratories" title="Chalk River Laboratories">Chalk River Laboratories</a>  in Ontario, Canada, and the <a href="/wiki/Petten_nuclear_reactor" title="Petten nuclear reactor">High Flux Reactor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Research_and_Consultancy_Group" title="Nuclear Research and Consultancy Group">Nuclear Research and Consultancy Group</a> in Petten, Netherlands. All major reactors that produce technetium-99m were built in the 1960s and are close to the <a href="/wiki/End-of-life_(product)" title="End-of-life (product)">end of life</a>. The two new Canadian <a href="/wiki/Multipurpose_Applied_Physics_Lattice_Experiment" title="Multipurpose Applied Physics Lattice Experiment">Multipurpose Applied Physics Lattice Experiment</a> reactors planned and built to produce 200% of the demand of technetium-99m relieved all other producers from building their own reactors. With the cancellation of the already tested reactors in 2008, the future supply of technetium-99m became problematic.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chalk River reactor was shut down for maintenance in August 2009, and reopened in August 2010. The Petten reactor had a 6-month scheduled maintenance shutdown on Friday, February 19, 2010, and reopened September 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> With millions of procedures relying on technetium-99m every year, the low supply has left a gap, leaving some practitioners to revert to techniques not used for 20 years. Somewhat allaying this issue is an announcement from the Polish <a href="/wiki/Maria_reactor" title="Maria reactor">Maria research reactor</a> that they have developed a technique to isolate technetium.<sup id="cite_ref-NY_Times_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY_Times-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_disposal">Waste disposal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Waste disposal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The long half-life of technetium-99 and its potential to form <a href="/wiki/Anionic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anionic">anionic</a> species creates a major concern for long-term <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">disposal of radioactive waste</a>. Many of the processes designed to remove fission products in reprocessing plants aim at <a href="/wiki/Cationic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cationic">cationic</a> species such as <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Strontium-90" title="Strontium-90">strontium-90</a>). Hence the pertechnetate escapes through those processes. Current disposal options favor <a href="/wiki/Geological_repository" class="mw-redirect" title="Geological repository">burial</a> in continental, geologically stable rock. The primary danger with such practice is the likelihood that the waste will contact water, which could leach radioactive contamination into the environment. The anionic pertechnetate and <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a> tend not to adsorb into the surfaces of minerals, and are likely to be washed away. By comparison <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a>  tend to bind to soil particles. Technetium could be immobilized by some environments, such as microbial activity in lake bottom sediments,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and the <b><a href="/wiki/Environmental_chemistry" title="Environmental chemistry">environmental chemistry</a></b> of technetium is an area of active research.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative disposal method, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a>, has been demonstrated at <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a> for technetium-99. In this process, the technetium (technetium-99 as a metal target) is bombarded with <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> to form the short-lived technetium-100 (half-life = 16&#160;seconds) which decays by beta decay to <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a>-100. If recovery of usable ruthenium is a goal, an extremely pure technetium target is needed; if small traces of the <a href="/wiki/Minor_actinide" title="Minor actinide">minor actinides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a> are present in the target, they are likely to undergo fission and form more <a href="/wiki/Fission_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Fission product">fission products</a> which increase the radioactivity of the irradiated target. The formation of ruthenium-106 (half-life 374&#160;days) from the 'fresh fission' is likely to increase the activity of the final ruthenium metal, which will then require a longer cooling time after irradiation before the ruthenium can be used.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The actual separation of technetium-99 from spent nuclear fuel is a long process. During <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">fuel reprocessing</a>, it comes out as a component of the highly radioactive waste liquid. After sitting for several years, the radioactivity reduces to a level where extraction of the long-lived isotopes, including technetium-99, becomes feasible. A series of chemical processes yields technetium-99 metal of high purity.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neutron_activation">Neutron activation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Neutron activation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Molybdenum-99" class="mw-redirect" title="Molybdenum-99">Molybdenum-99</a>, which decays to form technetium-99m, can be formed by the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a> of molybdenum-98.<sup id="cite_ref-IAEA_TECDOC-1340_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA_TECDOC-1340-80">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> When needed, other technetium isotopes are not produced in significant quantities by fission, but are manufactured by neutron irradiation of parent isotopes (for example, technetium-97 can be made by neutron irradiation of <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium-96" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruthenium-96">ruthenium-96</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Particle_accelerators">Particle accelerators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Particle accelerators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The feasibility of technetium-99m production with the 22-MeV-proton bombardment of a molybdenum-100 target in medical cyclotrons following the reaction <sup>100</sup>Mo(p,2n)<sup>99m</sup>Tc was demonstrated in 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The recent shortages of medical technetium-99m reignited the interest in its production by proton bombardment of isotopically-enriched (&gt;99.5%) molybdenum-100 targets.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20150530_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20150530-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Other techniques are being investigated for obtaining molybdenum-99 from molybdenum-100 via (n,2n) or (γ,n) reactions in particle accelerators.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_medicine_and_biology">Nuclear medicine and biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Nuclear medicine and biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">Technetium-99m</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:214px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basedow-vor-nach-RIT.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Upper image: two drop-like features merged at their bottoms; they have a yellow centre and a red rim on a black background. Caption: Graves&#39; Disease Tc-Uptake 16%. Lower image: red dots on black background. Caption: 250 Gy (30mCi) + Prednison." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Basedow-vor-nach-RIT.jpg" width="212" height="480" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="212" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basedow-vor-nach-RIT.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Technetium <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">scintigraphy</a> of a neck of <a href="/wiki/Graves%27_disease" title="Graves&#39; disease">Graves' disease</a> patient</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Technetium-99m" title="Technetium-99m">Technetium-99m</a> ("m" indicates that this is a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer#Metastable_isomers" title="Nuclear isomer">metastable</a> nuclear isomer) is used in radioactive isotope <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">medical tests</a>. For example, Technetium-99m is a <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracer</a> that medical imaging equipment tracks in the human body.<sup id="cite_ref-blocks_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blocks-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20150530_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20150530-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> It is well suited to the role because it emits readily detectable 140&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">keV</a> <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>, and its half-life is 6.01&#160;hours (meaning that about 94% of it decays to technetium-99 in 24&#160;hours).<sup id="cite_ref-enc_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enc-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The chemistry of technetium allows it to be bound to a variety of biochemical compounds, each of which determines how it is metabolized and deposited in the body, and this single isotope can be used for a multitude of diagnostic tests. More than 50 common <a href="/wiki/Radiopharmaceuticals" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiopharmaceuticals">radiopharmaceuticals</a> are based on technetium-99m for imaging and functional studies of the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a>, heart muscle, <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_lung" class="mw-redirect" title="Human lung">lungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gall_bladder" class="mw-redirect" title="Gall bladder">gall bladder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_skeleton" title="Human skeleton">skeleton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tumor" class="mw-redirect" title="Tumor">tumors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The longer-lived isotope, technetium-95m with a half-life of 61&#160;days, is used as a <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracer</a> to study the movement of technetium in the environment and in plant and animal systems.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_and_chemical">Industrial and chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Industrial and chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Technetium-99 decays almost entirely by beta decay, emitting beta particles with consistent low energies and no accompanying gamma rays. Moreover, its long half-life means that this emission decreases very slowly with time. It can also be extracted to a high chemical and isotopic purity from radioactive waste. For these reasons, it is a <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> (NIST) standard beta emitter, and is used for equipment calibration.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Technetium-99 has also been proposed for optoelectronic devices and <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanoscale</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear battery">nuclear batteries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a>, technetium can serve as a <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. In processes such as the <a href="/wiki/Dehydrogenation" title="Dehydrogenation">dehydrogenation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Isopropyl_alcohol" title="Isopropyl alcohol">isopropyl alcohol</a>, it is a far more effective catalyst than either rhenium or palladium. However, its radioactivity is a major problem in safe catalytic applications.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When steel is immersed in water, adding a small concentration (55&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parts_per_notation" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per notation">ppm</a>) of potassium pertechnetate(VII) to the water protects the <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> from corrosion, even if the temperature is raised to 250&#160;°C (523&#160;K).<sup id="cite_ref-corr_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corr-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> For this reason, pertechnetate has been used as an anodic <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> inhibitor for steel, although technetium's radioactivity poses problems that limit this application to self-contained systems.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> While (for example) <span class="chemf nowrap">CrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> can also inhibit corrosion, it requires a concentration ten times as high. In one experiment, a specimen of carbon steel was kept in an aqueous solution of pertechnetate for 20&#160;years and was still uncorroded.<sup id="cite_ref-corr_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corr-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The mechanism by which pertechnetate prevents corrosion is not well understood, but seems to involve the reversible formation of a thin surface layer (<a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>). One theory holds that the pertechnetate reacts with the steel surface to form a layer of technetium <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">dioxide</a> which prevents further corrosion; the same effect explains how iron powder can be used to remove pertechnetate from water. (<a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">Activated carbon</a> can also be used for the same purpose.) The effect disappears rapidly if the concentration of pertechnetate falls below the minimum concentration or if too high a concentration of other ions is added.<sup id="cite_ref-s91_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s91-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As noted, the radioactive nature of technetium (3&#160;MBq/L at the concentrations required) makes this corrosion protection impractical in almost all situations. Nevertheless, corrosion protection by pertechnetate ions was proposed (but never adopted) for use in <a href="/wiki/Boiling_water_reactor" title="Boiling water reactor">boiling water reactors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-s91_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s91-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Technetium plays no natural biological role and is not normally found in the human body.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Technetium is produced in quantity by nuclear fission, and spreads more readily than many radionuclides. It appears to have low chemical toxicity. For example, no significant change in blood formula, body and organ weights, and food consumption could be detected for rats which ingested up to 15&#160;µg of technetium-99 per gram of food for several weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The radiological toxicity of technetium (per unit of mass) is a function of compound, type of radiation for the isotope in question, and the isotope's half-life.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>All isotopes of technetium must be handled carefully. The most common isotope, technetium-99, is a weak beta emitter; such radiation is stopped by the walls of laboratory glassware.  The primary hazard when working with technetium is inhalation of dust; such <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a> in the lungs can pose a significant cancer risk. For most work, careful handling in a <a href="/wiki/Fume_hood" title="Fume hood">fume hood</a> is sufficient, and a <a href="/wiki/Glove_box" class="mw-redirect" title="Glove box">glove box</a> is not needed.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Technetium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

"Using first-principles X-ray-emission spectral-generation algorithms developed at NIST, I simulated the X-ray spectra that would be expected for Van Assche's initial estimates of the Noddacks' residue compositions. The first results were surprisingly close to their published spectrum! Over the next couple of years, we refined our reconstruction of their analytical methods and performed more sophisticated simulations. The agreement between simulated and reported spectra improved further. Our calculation of the amount of element&#160;43 required to produce their spectrum is quite similar to the direct measurements of natural technetium abundance in uranium ore published in 1999 by Dave Curtis and colleagues at Los Alamos. <li id="cite_note-Takahashi_et_al-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Takahashi_et_al_4-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Takahashi, K; Boyd, R.
